.mbox-text-span{margin-left:23px!important}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .ambox{margin:0 10%}}</style><table class="box-Essay-like plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-style ambox-essay-like" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><img alt="" data-file-height="48" data-file-width="48" decoding="async" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/40px-Edit-clear.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/60px-Edit-clear.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/80px-Edit-clear.svg.png 2x" width="40"/></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>is written like a <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:What_Wikipedia_is_not#Wikipedia_is_not_a_publisher_of_original_thought" title="Wikipedia:What Wikipedia is not">personal reflection, personal essay, or argumentative essay</a></b> that states a Wikipedia editor's personal feelings or presents an original argument about a topic.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Folk_music&amp;action=edit">help improve it</a> by rewriting it in an <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Writing_better_articles#Information_style_and_tone" title="Wikipedia:Writing better articles">encyclopedic style</a>.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">November 2016</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Korean_music-Nongak-03.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="533" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Korean_music-Nongak-03.jpg/220px-Korean_music-Nongak-03.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Korean_music-Nongak-03.jpg/330px-Korean_music-Nongak-03.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Korean_music-Nongak-03.jpg/440px-Korean_music-Nongak-03.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Korean_music-Nongak-03.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Korean traditional musicians</div></div></div>
<p>Music transmitted by word of mouth through a community, in time, develops many variants, because this kind of transmission cannot produce word-for-word and note-for-note accuracy. Indeed, many traditional singers are quite creative and deliberately modify the material they learn.
</p><p>For example, the words of "<a href="/wiki/I%27m_a_Man_You_Don%27t_Meet_Every_Day" title="I'm a Man You Don't Meet Every Day">I'm a Man You Don't Meet Every Day</a>" (Roud 975) are known from a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Broadside_(music)" title="Broadside (music)">broadside</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Bodleian_Library" title="Bodleian Library">Bodleian Library</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> The date is almost certainly before 1900, and it seems to be Irish. In 1958 the song was recorded in Canada (My Name is Pat and I'm Proud of That). Scottish traveler <a href="/wiki/Jeannie_Robertson" title="Jeannie Robertson">Jeannie Robertson</a> from Aberdeen, made the next recorded version in 1961. She has changed it to make reference to "Jock Stewart", one of her relatives, and there are no Irish references. In 1976 Scottish artist <a href="/wiki/Archie_Fisher" title="Archie Fisher">Archie Fisher</a> deliberately altered the song to remove the reference to a dog being shot. In 1985 <a href="/wiki/The_Pogues" title="The Pogues">The Pogues</a> took it full circle by restoring all the Irish references.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research" title="Wikipedia:No original research"><span title="The material near this tag possibly contains original research. (November 2016)">original research?</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Because variants proliferate naturally, it is naïve to believe that there is such a thing as the single "authentic" version of a <a href="/wiki/Ballad" title="Ballad">ballad</a> such as "<a href="/wiki/Barbara_Allen_(song)" title="Barbara Allen (song)">Barbara Allen</a>". Field researchers in traditional song (see below) have encountered countless versions of this ballad throughout the English-speaking world, and these versions often differ greatly from each other. None can reliably claim to be the original, and it is possible that the "original" version ceased to be sung centuries ago. Many versions can lay an equal claim to authenticity.
</p><p>The influential folklorist <a href="/wiki/Cecil_Sharp" title="Cecil Sharp">Cecil Sharp</a> felt that these competing variants of a traditional song would undergo a process of improvement akin to biological <a href="/wiki/Natural_selection" title="Natural selection">natural selection</a>: only those new variants that were the most appealing to ordinary singers would be picked up by others and transmitted onward in time. Thus, over time we would expect each traditional song to become aesthetically ever more appealing — it would be collectively composed to perfection, as it were, by the community.
</p><p>Literary interest in the popular ballad form dates back at least to <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Percy_(bishop_of_Dromore)" title="Thomas Percy (bishop of Dromore)">Thomas Percy</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_Wordsworth" title="William Wordsworth">William Wordsworth</a>. English Elizabethan and Stuart composers had often evolved their music from folk themes, the classical <a href="/wiki/Suite_(music)" title="Suite (music)">suite</a> was based upon stylised folk-dances, and <a href="/wiki/Haydn_and_folk_music" title="Haydn and folk music">Joseph Haydn</a>'s use of folk melodies is noted. But the emergence of the term "folk" coincided with an "outburst of national feeling all over Europe" that was particularly strong at the edges of Europe, where <a href="/wiki/Self-determination" title="Self-determination">national identity</a> was most asserted. <a href="/wiki/Musical_nationalism" title="Musical nationalism">Nationalist composers</a> emerged in Central Europe, Russia, Scandinavia, Spain and Britain: the music of <a href="/wiki/Anton%C3%ADn_Dvo%C5%99%C3%A1k" title="Antonín Dvořák">Dvořák</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bed%C5%99ich_Smetana" title="Bedřich Smetana">Smetana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edvard_Grieg" title="Edvard Grieg">Grieg</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rimsky-Korsakov" title="Rimsky-Korsakov">Rimsky-Korsakov</a>, <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Brahms" title="Johannes Brahms">Brahms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Franz_Liszt" title="Franz Liszt">Liszt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manuel_de_Falla" title="Manuel de Falla">de Falla</a>, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Wagner" title="Richard Wagner">Wagner</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jean_Sibelius" title="Jean Sibelius">Sibelius</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Vaughan_Williams" title="Ralph Vaughan Williams">Vaughan Williams</a>, <a href="/wiki/B%C3%A9la_Bart%C3%B3k" title="Béla Bartók">Bartók</a>, and many others drew upon folk melodies.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Regional_forms">Regional forms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Folk_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Regional forms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Naxi_Musicians_I.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1350" data-file-width="1800" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Naxi_Musicians_I.jpg/220px-Naxi_Musicians_I.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Naxi_Musicians_I.jpg/330px-Naxi_Musicians_I.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Naxi_Musicians_I.jpg/440px-Naxi_Musicians_I.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Naxi_Musicians_I.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Naxi traditional musicians</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:015_Die_Br%C3%BCder_Steinegger_nach_einem_Lichtbild_in_den_1880er-Jahren_-_%C3%84nderung_der_Tracht.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2012" data-file-width="1429" decoding="async" height="310" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/015_Die_Br%C3%BCder_Steinegger_nach_einem_Lichtbild_in_den_1880er-Jahren_-_%C3%84nderung_der_Tracht.jpg/220px-015_Die_Br%C3%BCder_Steinegger_nach_einem_Lichtbild_in_den_1880er-Jahren_-_%C3%84nderung_der_Tracht.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/015_Die_Br%C3%BCder_Steinegger_nach_einem_Lichtbild_in_den_1880er-Jahren_-_%C3%84nderung_der_Tracht.jpg/330px-015_Die_Br%C3%BCder_Steinegger_nach_einem_Lichtbild_in_den_1880er-Jahren_-_%C3%84nderung_der_Tracht.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/015_Die_Br%C3%BCder_Steinegger_nach_einem_Lichtbild_in_den_1880er-Jahren_-_%C3%84nderung_der_Tracht.jpg/440px-015_Die_Br%C3%BCder_Steinegger_nach_einem_Lichtbild_in_den_1880er-Jahren_-_%C3%84nderung_der_Tracht.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:015_Die_Br%C3%BCder_Steinegger_nach_einem_Lichtbild_in_den_1880er-Jahren_-_%C3%84nderung_der_Tracht.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Steinegger brothers, traditional fifers of <a href="/wiki/Grundlsee" title="Grundlsee">Grundlsee</a>, <a href="/wiki/Styria" title="Styria">Styria</a>, 1880</div></div></div>
<p>While the loss of traditional folk music in the face of the rise of <a href="/wiki/Popular_music" title="Popular music">popular music</a> is a worldwide phenomenon,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> it is not one occurring at a uniform rate throughout the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> The process is most advanced "where industrialization and <a href="/wiki/Commercialization" title="Commercialization">commercialisation</a> of culture are most advanced"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> but also occurs more gradually even in settings of lower technological advancement. However, the loss of traditional music is slowed in nations or regions where traditional folk music is a badge of cultural or national identity.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Early_folk_music.2C_fieldwork_and_scholarship"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_folk_music,_fieldwork_and_scholarship">Early folk music, fieldwork and scholarship</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Folk_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Early folk music, fieldwork and scholarship">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Much of what is known about folk music prior to the development of audio recording technology in the 19th century comes from <a href="/wiki/Field_research" title="Field research">fieldwork</a> and writings of scholars, collectors and proponents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="19th-century_Europe">19th-century Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Folk_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: 19th-century Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Starting in the 19th century, academics and amateur scholars, taking note of the musical traditions being lost, initiated various efforts to preserve the music of the people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> One such effort was the collection by <a href="/wiki/Francis_James_Child" title="Francis James Child">Francis James Child</a> in the late 19th century of the texts of over three hundred <a href="/wiki/Ballad" title="Ballad">ballads</a> in the English and Scots traditions (called the <a href="/wiki/Child_Ballads" title="Child Ballads">Child Ballads</a>), some of which predated the 16th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Donaldson,_2011_page_13_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-Donaldson,_2011_page_13-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>Contemporaneously with Child, the Reverend <a href="/wiki/Sabine_Baring-Gould" title="Sabine Baring-Gould">Sabine Baring-Gould</a> and later <a href="/wiki/Cecil_Sharp" title="Cecil Sharp">Cecil Sharp</a> worked to preserve a great body of English rural traditional song, music and dance, under the aegis of what became and remains the <a href="/wiki/English_Folk_Dance_and_Song_Society" title="English Folk Dance and Song Society">English Folk Dance and Song Society</a> (EFDSS).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> Sharp campaigned with some success to have English traditional songs (in his own heavily edited and expurgated versions) to be taught to school children in hopes of reviving and prolonging the popularity of those songs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> Throughout the 1960s and early to mid-1970s, American scholar <a href="/wiki/Bertrand_Harris_Bronson" title="Bertrand Harris Bronson">Bertrand Harris Bronson</a> published an exhaustive four-volume collection of the then-known variations of both the texts and tunes associated with what came to be known as the Child Canon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> He also advanced some significant theories concerning the workings of oral-aural tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>Similar activity was also under way in other countries. One of the most extensive was perhaps the work done in <a href="/wiki/Riga" title="Riga">Riga</a> by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Krisjanis_Barons" title="Krisjanis Barons">Krisjanis Barons</a>, who between the years 1894 and 1915 published six volumes that included the texts of 217,996 Latvian folk songs, the <i>Latvju dainas</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> In Norway the work of collectors such as <a href="/wiki/Ludvig_Mathias_Lindeman" title="Ludvig Mathias Lindeman">Ludvig Mathias Lindeman</a> was extensively used by Edvard Grieg in his <i>Lyric Pieces</i> for piano and in other works, which became immensely popular.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>Around this time, composers of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_classical_music" title="European classical music">classical music</a> developed a strong interest in collecting traditional songs, and a number of composers carried out their own field work on traditional music. These included <a href="/wiki/Percy_Grainger" title="Percy Grainger">Percy Grainger</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Vaughan_Williams" title="Ralph Vaughan Williams">Ralph Vaughan Williams</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> in England and <a href="/wiki/B%C3%A9la_Bart%C3%B3k" title="Béla Bartók">Béla Bartók</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> in Hungary. These composers, like many of their predecessors, both made arrangements of folk songs and incorporated traditional material into original classical compositions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="North_America">North America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Folk_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: North America">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cecil-sharp-appalachia-map.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="297" data-file-width="462" decoding="async" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Cecil-sharp-appalachia-map.png/350px-Cecil-sharp-appalachia-map.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/31/Cecil-sharp-appalachia-map.png 1.5x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cecil-sharp-appalachia-map.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Locations in Southern and Central Appalachia visited by the British folklorist <a href="/wiki/Cecil_Sharp" title="Cecil Sharp">Cecil Sharp</a> in 1916 (blue), 1917 (green), and 1918 (red). Sharp sought "old world" English and Scottish ballads passed down to the region's inhabitants from their British ancestors. He collected hundreds of such ballads, the most productive areas being the Blue Ridge Mountains of North Carolina and the Cumberland Mountains of Kentucky.</div></div></div>
<p>The advent of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Audio_recording" title="Audio recording">audio recording</a> technology provided folklorists with a revolutionary tool to preserve vanishing musical forms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> The earliest American folk music scholars were with the <a href="/wiki/American_Folklore_Society" title="American Folklore Society">American Folklore Society</a> (AFS), which emerged in the late 1800s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> Their studies expanded to include <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_music_of_North_America" title="Indigenous music of North America">Native American music</a>, but still treated folk music as a historical item preserved in isolated societies as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> In North America, during the 1930s and 1940s, the <a href="/wiki/Library_of_Congress" title="Library of Congress">Library of Congress</a> worked through the offices of traditional music collectors <a href="/wiki/Robert_Winslow_Gordon" title="Robert Winslow Gordon">Robert Winslow Gordon</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Alan_Lomax" title="Alan Lomax">Alan Lomax</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> and others to capture as much North American field material as possible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_Lomax" title="John Lomax">John Lomax</a> (the father of Alan Lomax) was the first prominent scholar to study distinctly American folk music such as that of cowboys and southern blacks. His first major published work was in 1911, <i>Cowboy Songs and Other Frontier Ballads</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> and was arguably the most prominent US folk music scholar of his time, notably during the beginnings of the folk music revival in the 1930s and early 1940s. Cecil Sharp also worked in America, recording the traditional songs of the Appalachian Mountains in 1916–1918 in collaboration with <a href="/wiki/Maud_Karpeles" title="Maud Karpeles">Maud Karpeles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Olive_Dame_Campbell" title="Olive Dame Campbell">Olive Dame Campbell</a> and is considered the first major scholar covering American folk music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> Campbell and Sharp are represented under other names by actors in the modern movie <i><a href="/wiki/Songcatcher" title="Songcatcher">Songcatcher</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>One strong theme amongst folk scholars in the early decades of the 20th century was <a href="/wiki/Regionalism_(art)" title="Regionalism (art)">regionalism</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> the analysis of the diversity of folk music (and related cultures) based on regions of the US rather than based on a given song's historical roots.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> Later, a dynamic of class and circumstances was added to this.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> The most prominent regionalists were literary figures with a particular interest in folklore.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Carl_Sandburg" title="Carl Sandburg">Carl Sandburg</a> often traveled the U.S. as a writer and a poet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> He also collected songs in his travels and, in 1927, published them in the book <i><a href="/wiki/The_American_Songbag" title="The American Songbag">The American Songbag</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> Rachel Donaldson, a historian who worked for Vanderbilt, later stated this about The American Songbird in her analysis of the folk music revival. "In his collections of folk songs, Sandburg added a class dynamic to popular understandings of American folk music. This was the final element of the foundation upon which the early folk music revivalists constructed their own view of Americanism. Sandburg's working class Americans joined with the <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_group" title="Ethnic group">ethnically</a>, <a href="/wiki/Race_(human_categorization)" title="Race (human categorization)">racially</a>, and regionally diverse citizens that other scholars, public intellectuals, and folklorists celebrated their own definitions of the American folk, definitions that the folk revivalists used in constructing their own understanding of American folk music, and an overarching American identity".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p>Prior to the 1930s, the study of folk music was primarily the province of scholars and collectors. The 1930s saw the beginnings of larger scale themes, commonalities, and linkages in folk music developing in the populace and practitioners as well, often related to the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Donaldson,_2011,_p_39-55_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Donaldson,_2011,_p_39-55-92">[92]</a></sup> Regionalism and <a href="/wiki/Cultural_pluralism" title="Cultural pluralism">cultural pluralism</a> grew as influences and themes. During this time folk music began to become enmeshed with political and social activism themes and movements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Donaldson,_2011,_p_39-55_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-Donaldson,_2011,_p_39-55-92">[92]</a></sup> Two related developments were the U.S. Communist Party's interest in folk music as a way to reach and influence Americans,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> and politically active prominent folk musicians and scholars seeing communism as a possible better system, through the lens of the Great Depression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Woody_Guthrie" title="Woody Guthrie">Woody Guthrie</a> exemplifies songwriters and artists with such an outlook.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup>
</p><p>Folk music festivals proliferated during the 1930s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/President_Franklin_Roosevelt" title="President Franklin Roosevelt">President Franklin Roosevelt</a> was a fan of folk music, hosted folk concerts at the <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a>, and often patronized folk festivals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> One prominent festival was <a href="/wiki/Sarah_Gertrude_Knott" title="Sarah Gertrude Knott">Sarah Gertrude Knott</a>'s <a href="/wiki/National_Folk_Festival_(United_States)" title="National Folk Festival (United States)">National Folk Festival</a>, established in St. Louis, Missouri in 1934.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> Under the sponsorship of <a href="/wiki/The_Washington_Post" title="The Washington Post">the Washington Post</a>, the festival was held in Washington, DC at Constitution Hall from 1937 to 1942.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> The folk music movement, festivals, and the wartime effort were seen as forces for social goods such as democracy, cultural pluralism, and the removal of culture and race-based barriers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097025294">.mw-parser-output .side-box{margin:4px 0;box-sizing:border-box;border:1px solid #aaa;font-size:88%;line-height:1.25em;background-color:#f9f9f9}.mw-parser-output .side-box-abovebelow,.mw-parser-output .side-box-text{padding:0.25em 0.9em}.mw-parser-output .side-box-image{padding:2px 0 2px 0.9em;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .side-box-imageright{padding:2px 0.9em 2px 0;text-align:center}@media(min-width:500px){.mw-parser-output .side-box-flex{display:flex;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .side-box-text{flex:1}}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .side-box{width:238px}.mw-parser-output .side-box-right{clear:right;float:right;margin-left:1em}.mw-parser-output .side-box-left{margin-right:1em}}</style><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096940132">.mw-parser-output .listen .side-box-text{line-height:1.1em}.mw-parser-output .listen-plain{border:none;background:transparent}.mw-parser-output .listen-embedded{width:100%;margin:0;border-width:1px 0 0 0;background:transparent}.mw-parser-output .listen-header{padding:2px}.mw-parser-output .listen-embedded .listen-header{padding:2px 0}.mw-parser-output .listen-file-header{padding:4px 0}.mw-parser-output .listen .description{padding-top:2px}.mw-parser-output .listen .mw-tmh-player{max-width:100%}@media(max-width:719px){.mw-parser-output .listen{clear:both}}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .listen:not(.listen-noimage){width:320px}.mw-parser-output .listen-left{overflow:visible;float:left}.mw-parser-output .listen-center{float:none;margin-left:auto;margin-right:auto}}</style><div class="side-box side-box-right listen noprint">
<div class="side-box-flex">
<div class="side-box-image"><div class="center"><div class="floatnone"><img alt="" data-file-height="160" data-file-width="160" decoding="async" height="50" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></div></div></div>
<div class="side-box-text plainlist"><div class="haudio">
<div class="listen-file-header"><a href="/wiki/File:Barbara_Allen.ogg" title="File:Barbara Allen.ogg">Barbara Allen</a></div>
<div><audio controls="" data-durationhint="160" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="Barbara_Allen.ogg" id="mwe_player_0" preload="none" style="width:232px;" width="232"><source data-bandwidth="69035" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-title="Original Ogg file (69 kbps)" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/ad/Barbara_Allen.ogg" type='audio/ogg; codecs="vorbis"'/><source data-bandwidth="74648" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-title="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/a/ad/Barbara_Allen.ogg/Barbara_Allen.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg"/></audio></div>
<div class="description"><i><a href="/wiki/Barbara_Allen_(song)" title="Barbara Allen (song)">Barbara Allen</a></i>, a traditional English language folk ballad, sung by Hule "Queen" Hines of Florida to <a href="/wiki/John_Lomax" title="John Lomax">John Lomax</a> in 1939</div></div></div></div>
<div class="side-box-abovebelow"><hr/><i class="selfreference">Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Help:Media" title="Help:Media">media help</a>.</i></div>
</div><p>The American folk music revivalists of the 1930s approached folk music in different ways.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Donaldson87_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Donaldson87-101">[101]</a></sup> Three primary schools of thought emerged: "Traditionalists" (e.g. Sarah Gertrude Knott and <a href="/wiki/John_Lomax" title="John Lomax">John Lomax</a>) emphasized the preservation of songs as artifacts of deceased cultures. "Functional" folklorists (e.g. Botkin and Alan Lomax) maintained that songs only retain relevance when used by those cultures which retain the traditions which birthed those songs. "Left-wing" folk revivalists (e.g. Charles Seeger and Lawrence Gellert) emphasized music's role "in 'people's' struggles for social and political rights".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Donaldson87_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-Donaldson87-101">[101]</a></sup> By the end of the 1930s these and others had turned American folk music into a social movement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Donaldson87_101-2"><a href="#cite_note-Donaldson87-101">[101]</a></sup>
</p><p>Sometimes folk musicians became scholars and advocates themselves. For example, <a href="/wiki/Jean_Ritchie" title="Jean Ritchie">Jean Ritchie</a> (1922–2015) was the youngest child of a large family from <a href="/wiki/Viper,_Kentucky" title="Viper, Kentucky">Viper, Kentucky</a> that had preserved many of the old <a href="/wiki/Appalachia" title="Appalachia">Appalachian</a> traditional songs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> Ritchie, living in a time when the <a href="/wiki/Appalachian_Mountains" title="Appalachian Mountains">Appalachians</a> had opened up to outside influence, was university educated and ultimately moved to New York City, where she made a number of classic recordings of the family repertoire and published an important compilation of these songs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup>
</p><p>In January 2012, the <a href="/wiki/American_Folklife_Center" title="American Folklife Center">American Folklife Center</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Library_of_Congress" title="Library of Congress">Library of Congress</a>, with the Association for Cultural Equity, announced that they would release Lomax's vast archive of 1946 and later recording in digital form. Lomax spent the last 20 years of his life working on an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interactive_Multimedia" title="Interactive Multimedia">Interactive Multimedia</a> educational computer project he called the <a href="/wiki/Cantometrics#Cantometrics_as_an_educational_tool" title="Cantometrics">Global Jukebox</a>, which included 5,000 hours of sound recordings, 400,000 feet of film, 3,000 <a href="/wiki/Videotape" title="Videotape">videotapes</a>, and 5,000 photographs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> As of March 2012, this has been accomplished. Approximately 17,400 of Lomax's recordings from 1946 and later have been made available free online.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ACEmusic_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-ACEmusic-105">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NPR17000_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-NPR17000-106">[106]</a></sup> This material from Alan Lomax's independent archive, begun in 1946, which has been digitized and offered by the Association for Cultural Equity, is "distinct from the thousands of earlier recordings on acetate and aluminum discs he made from 1933 to 1942 under the auspices of the Library of Congress. This earlier collection—which includes the famous Jelly Roll Morton, Woody Guthrie, Lead Belly, and Muddy Waters sessions, as well as Lomax's prodigious collections made in <a href="/wiki/Haiti" title="Haiti">Haiti</a> and Eastern Kentucky (1937) — is the provenance of the American Folklife Center"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ACEmusic_105-1"><a href="#cite_note-ACEmusic-105">[105]</a></sup> at the library of Congress.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="National_and_regional_forms">National and regional forms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Folk_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: National and regional forms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Africa">Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Folk_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Africa" title="Music of Africa">Music of Africa</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mbira_dzavadzimu.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1660" data-file-width="1722" decoding="async" height="212" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Mbira_dzavadzimu.jpg/220px-Mbira_dzavadzimu.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Mbira_dzavadzimu.jpg/330px-Mbira_dzavadzimu.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Mbira_dzavadzimu.jpg/440px-Mbira_dzavadzimu.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mbira_dzavadzimu.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The African <a href="/wiki/Lamellophone" title="Lamellophone">lamellophone</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thumb_piano" title="Thumb piano">thumb piano</a> or <a href="/wiki/Mbira" title="Mbira">mbira</a></div></div></div>
<p>Africa is a vast continent<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> and its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Regions_of_Africa" title="Regions of Africa">regions</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_African_countries" title="List of African countries">nations</a> have distinct musical traditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Middle_Eastern_and_North_African_folk_music_traditions" title="List of Middle Eastern and North African folk music traditions">music of North Africa</a> for the most part has a different history from <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_African_music_traditions" title="Sub-Saharan African music traditions">Sub-Saharan African music traditions</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup>
</p><p>The music and dance forms of the <a href="/wiki/African_diaspora" title="African diaspora">African diaspora</a>, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African_American_music" title="African American music">African American music</a> and many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caribbean_music" title="Caribbean music">Caribbean</a> genres like <a href="/wiki/Soca_music" title="Soca music">soca</a>, <a href="/wiki/Calypso_music" title="Calypso music">calypso</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zouk_(musical_movement)" title="Zouk (musical movement)">Zouk</a>; and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_American_music" title="Latin American music">Latin American music</a> genres like the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Samba_(music)" title="Samba (music)">samba</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cuban_rumba" title="Cuban rumba">Cuban rumba</a>, <a href="/wiki/Salsa_music" title="Salsa music">salsa</a>; and other <a href="/wiki/Clave_(rhythm)" title="Clave (rhythm)">clave (rhythm)</a>-based genres, were founded to varying degrees on the music of <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_Africa" title="Slavery in Africa">African slaves</a>, which has in turn influenced <a href="/wiki/African_popular_music" title="African popular music">African popular music</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Asia">Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Folk_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Tamang_Selo" title="Tamang Selo">Tamang Selo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iranian_folk_music" title="Iranian folk music">Iranian folk music</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Indian_folk_music" title="Indian folk music">Indian folk music</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Paban_Das_Baul_at_Nine_Lives_concert,_2009.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1417" data-file-width="2128" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Paban_Das_Baul_at_Nine_Lives_concert%2C_2009.jpg/220px-Paban_Das_Baul_at_Nine_Lives_concert%2C_2009.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Paban_Das_Baul_at_Nine_Lives_concert%2C_2009.jpg/330px-Paban_Das_Baul_at_Nine_Lives_concert%2C_2009.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Paban_Das_Baul_at_Nine_Lives_concert%2C_2009.jpg/440px-Paban_Das_Baul_at_Nine_Lives_concert%2C_2009.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Paban_Das_Baul_at_Nine_Lives_concert,_2009.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Paban_Das_Baul" title="Paban Das Baul">Paban Das Baul</a>, <a href="/wiki/Baul" title="Baul">baul</a> singer at Nine Lives concert, 2009</div></div></div>
<p>Many Asian civilizations distinguish between <a href="/wiki/Art_music" title="Art music">art</a>/court/classical styles and "folk" music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> For example, the late <a href="/wiki/Alam_Lohar" title="Alam Lohar">Alam Lohar</a> is an example of a South Asian singer who was classified as a folk singer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup>
</p><p>Khunung Eshei/Khuland Eshei is an ancient folk song from India, a country of Asia, of <a href="/wiki/Meitei_people" title="Meitei people">Meiteis</a> of <a href="/wiki/Manipur" title="Manipur">Manipur</a>, that is an example of Asian folk music, and how they put it into its own genre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Folk_music_of_China">Folk music of China</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Folk_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Folk music of China">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Music_of_China#Folk_music" title="Music of China">Music of China § Folk music</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shijing" title="Shijing">Shijing</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Yuefu" title="Yuefu">Yuefu</a></div>
<p>Archaeological discoveries date <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Culture_of_China" title="Culture of China">Chinese</a> folk music back 7000 years;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> it is largely based on the <a href="/wiki/Pentatonic_scale" title="Pentatonic scale">pentatonic scale</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han</a> traditional weddings and funerals usually include a form of oboe called a <a href="/wiki/Suona" title="Suona">suona</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> and apercussive ensembles called a <a href="/wiki/Chuigushou" title="Chuigushou">chuigushou</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> Ensembles consisting of <a href="/wiki/Mouth_organ" title="Mouth organ">mouth organs</a> (<a href="/wiki/Sheng_(instrument)" title="Sheng (instrument)">sheng</a>), shawms (<a href="/wiki/Suona" title="Suona">suona</a>), flutes (<a href="/wiki/Dizi_(instrument)" title="Dizi (instrument)">dizi</a>) and percussion instruments (especially <a href="/wiki/Yunluo" title="Yunluo">yunluo</a> <a href="/wiki/Gong" title="Gong">gongs</a>) are popular in northern villages;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> their music is descended from the imperial temple music of Beijing, <a href="/wiki/Xi%27an" title="Xi'an">Xi'an</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wutai_shan" title="Wutai shan">Wutai shan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tianjin" title="Tianjin">Tianjin</a>. <a href="/wiki/Xi%27an" title="Xi'an">Xi'an</a> drum music, consisting of wind and percussive instruments,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> is popular around Xi'an, and has received some commercial popularity outside of China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> Another important instrument is the <a href="/wiki/Sheng_(instrument)" title="Sheng (instrument)">sheng</a>, a type of Chinese <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_flute" title="Chinese flute">pipe</a>, an ancient instrument that is ancestor of all Western <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Free_reed" title="Free reed">free reed</a> instruments, such as the <a href="/wiki/Accordion" title="Accordion">accordion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> Parades led by Western-type <a href="/wiki/Brass_band" title="Brass band">brass bands</a> are common, often competing in volume with a shawm/chuigushou band.
</p><p>In southern <a href="/wiki/Fujian" title="Fujian">Fujian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a>, Nanyin or <a href="/wiki/Nanguan_music" title="Nanguan music">Nanguan</a> is a genre of traditional ballads.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> They are sung by a woman accompanied by a <a href="/wiki/Xiao_(flute)" title="Xiao (flute)">xiao</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Pipa" title="Pipa">pipa</a>, as well as other traditional instruments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> The music is generally sorrowful and typically deals with love-stricken people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> Further south, in <a href="/wiki/Shantou" title="Shantou">Shantou</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hakka_people" title="Hakka people">Hakka</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chaozhou" title="Chaozhou">Chaozhou</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guzheng" title="Guzheng">zheng</a> ensembles are popular.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sizhu" title="Sizhu">Sizhu</a> ensembles use flutes and bowed or plucked string instruments to make harmonious and melodious music that has become popular in the West among some listeners.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> These are popular in <a href="/wiki/Nanjing" title="Nanjing">Nanjing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hangzhou" title="Hangzhou">Hangzhou</a>, as well as elsewhere along the southern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yangtze_River" title="Yangtze River">Yangtze</a> area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jiangnan_Sizhu" title="Jiangnan Sizhu">Jiangnan Sizhu</a> (silk and bamboo music from <a href="/wiki/Jiangnan" title="Jiangnan">Jiangnan</a>) is a style of instrumental music, often played by amateur musicians in tea houses in Shanghai.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Guangdong_music_(genre)" title="Guangdong music (genre)">Guangdong Music</a> or <a href="/wiki/Guangdong_music_(genre)" title="Guangdong music (genre)">Cantonese Music</a> is instrumental music from <a href="/wiki/Guangzhou" title="Guangzhou">Guangzhou</a> and surrounding areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> The music from this region influenced <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cantonese_Opera" title="Cantonese Opera">Yueju</a> (Cantonese Opera) music,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> which would later grow popular during the self-described "Golden Age" of China under the <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">PRC</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Traditional_folk_music_of_Sri_Lanka">Traditional folk music of Sri Lanka</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Folk_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Traditional folk music of Sri Lanka">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Sri_Lanka" title="Music of Sri Lanka">Music of Sri Lanka</a></div>
<p>The art, music and dance of Sri Lanka derive from the elements of nature, and have been enjoyed and developed in the <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhist</a> environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> The music is of several types and uses only a few types of instruments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> The folk songs and poems were used in social gatherings to work together. The Indian influenced classical music has grown to be unique.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> The traditional drama, music and songs of Sinhala Light Music are typically Sri Lankan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup>
The temple paintings and carvings feature birds, elephants, wild animals, flowers, and trees, and the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Indian_folk_dances" title="List of Indian folk dances">Traditional 18 Dances</a> display the dancing of birds and animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> For example:
</p>
<ul><li>Mayura Wannama – The dance of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peacock" title="Peacock">peacock</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup></li>
<li>Hanuma Wannama – The dance of the monkey<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup></li>
<li>Gajaga Wannama – The dance of the elephant</li></ul>
<p>Musical types include:
</p>
<ul><li>Local drama music includes <a href="/wiki/Kolam" title="Kolam">Kolam</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> and Nadagam types.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> Kolam music is based on low country tunes primarily to accompany mask dance in <a href="/wiki/Exorcism" title="Exorcism">exorcism</a> rituals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> It is considered less developed/evolved, true to the folk tradition and a preserving of a more ancient artform.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> It is limited to approximately 3–4 notes and is used by the ordinary people for pleasure and entertainment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup></li>
<li>Nadagam music is a more developed form of drama influenced from South Indian street drama which was introduced by some south Indian artists. Phillippu Singho from Negombo in 1824 performed "Harishchandra Nadagama" in <a href="/wiki/Hanguranketha_Divisional_Secretariat" title="Hanguranketha Divisional Secretariat">Hnguranketha</a> which was originally written in the <a href="/wiki/Telugu_language" title="Telugu language">Telingu</a> language. Later "Maname",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> "Sanda kinduru"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> and others were introduced. Don Bastian of <a href="/wiki/Dehiwala" title="Dehiwala">Dehiwala</a> introduced Noorthy firstly by looking at Indian dramas and then <a href="/wiki/John_de_Silva" title="John de Silva">John de Silva</a> developed it as did Ramayanaya in 1886.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup></li>
<li>Sinhala light music is currently the most popular type of music in Sri Lanka and enriched with the influence of folk music, kolam music, nadagam music, noorthy music, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Film_music" title="Film music">film music</a>, classical music, Western music, and others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup> Some artists visited India to learn music and later started introducing light music. <a href="/wiki/Ananda_Samarakoon" title="Ananda Samarakoon">Ananda Samarakone</a> was the pioneer of this<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup> and also composed the national anthem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>The classical Sinhalese orchestra consists of five categories of instruments, but among the percussion instruments, the drum is essential for dance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup>
The vibrant beat of the rhythm of the drums form the basic of the dance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup> The dancers' feet bounce off the floor and they leap and swirl in patterns that reflect the complex rhythms of the drum beat. This drum beat may seem simple on the first hearing but it takes a long time to master the intricate rhythms and variations, which the drummer sometimes can bring to a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crescendo" title="Crescendo">crescendo</a> of intensity. There are six common types of drums falling within 3 styles (one-faced, two-faced, and flat-faced):<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>The typical Sinhala Dance is identified as the <a href="/wiki/Kandy" title="Kandy">Kandyan</a> dance and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geta_Bera" title="Geta Bera">Gatabera</a> drum is indispensable to this dance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup></li>
<li>Yak-bera is the demon drum or the drum used in low country dance in which the dancers wear masks and perform devil dancing, which has become a highly developed form of art.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup></li>
<li>The Daula is a <a href="/wiki/Barrel" title="Barrel">barrel</a>-shaped drum, and it was used as a companion drum with a Thammattama in the past, to keep strict time with the beat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thammattama" title="Thammattama">Thammattama</a> is a flat, two-faced drum. The drummer strikes the drum on the two surfaces on top with sticks, unlike the others where you drum on the sides. This is a companion drum to the aforementioned Dawula.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup></li>
<li>A small double-headed hand drum is used to accompany songs. It is primarily heard in the poetry dances like vannam.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="What is the name of the drum? Giving a description doesn't provide the name. (October 2021)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup></li>
<li>The Rabana is a flat-faced circular drum and comes in several sizes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup> The large Rabana - called the Banku Rabana - has to be placed on the floor like a circular short-legged <a href="/wiki/Table_(furniture)" title="Table (furniture)">table</a> and several people (traditionally women) can sit around it and beat on it with both hands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup> This is used in festivals such as the <a href="/wiki/Sinhalese_New_Year" title="Sinhalese New Year">Sinhalese New Year</a> and ceremonies such as weddings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup> The resounding beat of the Rabana symbolizes the joyous moods of the occasion. The small Rabana is a form of mobile drum beat since the player carries it wherever the person goes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>Other instruments include:
</p>
<ul><li>The Thalampata – 2 small cymbals joined by a string.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup></li>
<li>The wind section, is dominated by an instrument akin to the clarinet.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="Name of wind instrument described? (October 2021)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> This is not normally used for dances. This is important to note because the Sinhalese dance is not set to music as the western world knows it; rhythm is king.</li>
<li>The flutes of metal such as silver &amp; brass produce <a href="/wiki/Shrillness" title="Shrillness">shrill</a> music to accompany Kandyan Dances, while the plaintive strains of music of the reed flute may pierce the air in devil-dancing. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conch-shell" title="Conch-shell">conch-shell</a> (<a href="/wiki/Hakgediya" title="Hakgediya">Hakgediya</a>) is another form of a natural instrument, and the player blows it to announce the opening of ceremonies of grandeur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Ravanahatha" title="Ravanahatha">Ravanahatha</a> (ravanhatta, rawanhattha, ravanastron or ravana hasta veena) is a bowed fiddle that was once popular in Western India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup> It is believed to have originated among the Hela civilisation of Sri Lanka in the time of King Ravana.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FSL_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-FSL-176">[176]</a></sup> The bowl is made of cut coconut shell, the mouth of which is covered with goat hide. A dandi, made of bamboo, is attached to this shell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FSL_176-1"><a href="#cite_note-FSL-176">[176]</a></sup> The principal strings are two: one of steel and the other of a set of horsehair. The long bow has jingle bells<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dailynews.lk_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-dailynews.lk-178">[178]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Australia">Australia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Folk_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Australia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Australian_folk_music" title="Australian folk music">Australian folk music</a> and <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_music_of_Australia" title="Indigenous music of Australia">Indigenous music of Australia</a></div>
<p>Folk song traditions were taken to Australia by early settlers from England, Scotland and Ireland and gained particular foothold in the rural <a href="/wiki/Outback" title="Outback">outback</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup> The rhyming songs, poems and tales written in the form of <a href="/wiki/Bush_ballad" title="Bush ballad">bush ballads</a> often relate to the itinerant and rebellious spirit of Australia in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Bush" title="The Bush">The Bush</a>, and the authors and performers are often referred to as bush bards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abc.net.au_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-abc.net.au-181">[181]</a></sup> The 19th century was the golden age of bush ballads.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup> Several collectors have catalogued the songs including <a href="/wiki/John_Meredith_(folklorist)" title="John Meredith (folklorist)">John Meredith</a> whose recording in the 1950s became the basis of the collection in the <a href="/wiki/National_Library_of_Australia" title="National Library of Australia">National Library of Australia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abc.net.au_181-1"><a href="#cite_note-abc.net.au-181">[181]</a></sup>
</p><p>The songs tell personal stories of life in the wide open country of Australia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup> Typical subjects include mining, raising and droving cattle, <a href="/wiki/Sheep_shearing" title="Sheep shearing">sheep shearing</a>, wanderings, war stories, the <a href="/wiki/1891_Australian_shearers%27_strike" title="1891 Australian shearers' strike">1891 Australian shearers' strike</a>, class conflicts between the landless working class and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Squatting_(pastoral)" title="Squatting (pastoral)">squatters</a> (landowners), and outlaws such as <a href="/wiki/Ned_Kelly" title="Ned Kelly">Ned Kelly</a>, as well as love interests and more modern fare such as <a href="/wiki/Truck_driver" title="Truck driver">trucking</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup> The most famous bush ballad is "<a href="/wiki/Waltzing_Matilda" title="Waltzing Matilda">Waltzing Matilda</a>", which has been called "the unofficial national anthem of Australia".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indigenous_Australian_music" title="Indigenous Australian music">Indigenous Australian music</a> includes the music of <a href="/wiki/Aboriginal_Australians" title="Aboriginal Australians">Aboriginal Australians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Torres_Strait_Islanders" title="Torres Strait Islanders">Torres Strait Islanders</a>, who are collectively called <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_Australians" title="Indigenous Australians">Indigenous Australians</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup> it incorporates a variety of distinctive traditional music styles practiced by Indigenous Australian peoples, as well as a range of contemporary musical styles of and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fusion_(music)" title="Fusion (music)">fusion</a> with European traditions as interpreted and performed by indigenous Australian artists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup> Music has formed an integral part of the <a href="/wiki/Society" title="Society">social</a>, <a href="/wiki/Culture" title="Culture">cultural</a> and ceremonial observances of these peoples, down through the millennia of their individual and collective histories to the present day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup> The traditional forms include many aspects of performance and <a href="/wiki/Musical_instrument" title="Musical instrument">musical instruments</a> unique to particular regions or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Indigenous_Australian_group_names" title="List of Indigenous Australian group names">Indigenous Australian groups</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup> Equal elements of musical tradition are common through much of the Australian continent, and even beyond.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup> The culture of the Torres Strait Islanders is related to that of adjacent parts of <a href="/wiki/New_Guinea" title="New Guinea">New Guinea</a> and so their music is also related. Music is a vital part of Indigenous Australians' cultural maintenance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Europe">Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Folk_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/English_folk_music" title="English folk music">English folk music</a>, <a href="/wiki/French_folk_music" title="French folk music">French folk music</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German_folk_music" title="German folk music">German folk music</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_folk_music" title="Greek folk music">Greek folk music</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Italian_folk_music" title="Italian folk music">Italian folk music</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2012-01-25_BattlefieldBand_0097.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1450" data-file-width="2260" decoding="async" height="192" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/2012-01-25_BattlefieldBand_0097.JPG/300px-2012-01-25_BattlefieldBand_0097.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/2012-01-25_BattlefieldBand_0097.JPG/450px-2012-01-25_BattlefieldBand_0097.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/2012-01-25_BattlefieldBand_0097.JPG/600px-2012-01-25_BattlefieldBand_0097.JPG 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2012-01-25_BattlefieldBand_0097.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Battlefield_Band" title="Battlefield Band">Battlefield Band</a> performing in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freiburg" title="Freiburg">Freiburg</a> in 2012</div></div></div>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Celtic_traditional_music">Celtic traditional music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Folk_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Celtic traditional music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Folk_music_of_Ireland" title="Folk music of Ireland">Folk music of Ireland</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Folk_music_of_Scotland" title="Folk music of Scotland">Folk music of Scotland</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Celtic_music" title="Celtic music">Celtic music</a> is a term used by artists, record companies, music stores and music magazines to describe a broad grouping of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Musical_genre" title="Musical genre">musical genres</a> that evolved out of the folk musical traditions of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Celtic_people" title="Celtic people">Celtic peoples</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup> These traditions include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Folk_music_of_Ireland" title="Folk music of Ireland">Irish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Scotland" title="Music of Scotland">Scottish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Music_of_the_Isle_of_Man" title="Music of the Isle of Man">Manx</a>, <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Cornwall" title="Music of Cornwall">Cornish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Wales" title="Music of Wales">Welsh</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Breton_music" title="Breton music">Breton</a> traditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Music_of_Galicia,_Cantabria_and_Asturias" title="Music of Galicia, Cantabria and Asturias">Asturian and Galician music</a> is often included, though there is no significant research showing that this has any close musical relationship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Brittany" title="Music of Brittany">Brittany</a>'s Folk revival began in the 1950s with the "bagadoù" and the "kan-ha-diskan" before growing to world fame through <a href="/wiki/Alan_Stivell" title="Alan Stivell">Alan Stivell</a>'s work since the mid-1960s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-saw_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-saw-198">[198]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Ireland, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Clancy_Brothers_and_Tommy_Makem" title="The Clancy Brothers and Tommy Makem">The Clancy Brothers and Tommy Makem</a> (although its members were all Irish-born, the group became famous while based in New York's Greenwich Village<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup>), <a href="/wiki/The_Dubliners" title="The Dubliners">The Dubliners</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clannad_(musical_group)" title="Clannad (musical group)">Clannad</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Planxty" title="Planxty">Planxty</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[202]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/The_Chieftains" title="The Chieftains">The Chieftains</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[203]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[204]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/The_Pogues" title="The Pogues">The Pogues</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[205]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/The_Corrs" title="The Corrs">The Corrs</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[206]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/The_Irish_Rovers" title="The Irish Rovers">The Irish Rovers</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[207]</a></sup> and a variety of other folk bands have done much over the past few decades to revitalise and re-popularise <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Folk_music_of_Ireland" title="Folk music of Ireland">Irish traditional music</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[208]</a></sup> These bands were rooted, to a greater or lesser extent, in a tradition of Irish music and benefited from the efforts of artists such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seamus_Ennis" title="Seamus Ennis">Seamus Ennis</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peter_Douglas_Kennedy" title="Peter Douglas Kennedy">Peter Kennedy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-saw_198-1"><a href="#cite_note-saw-198">[198]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Corries" title="The Corries">The Corries</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[209]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Silly_Wizard" title="Silly Wizard">Silly Wizard</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[210]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[211]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Capercaillie_(band)" title="Capercaillie (band)">Capercaillie</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[212]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Runrig" title="Runrig">Runrig</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[213]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jackie_Leven" title="Jackie Leven">Jackie Leven</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[214]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Julie_Fowlis" title="Julie Fowlis">Julie Fowlis</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[215]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Karine_Polwart" title="Karine Polwart">Karine Polwart</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[216]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Alasdair_Roberts_(musician)" title="Alasdair Roberts (musician)">Alasdair Roberts</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[217]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218"><a href="#cite_note-218">[218]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Dick_Gaughan" title="Dick Gaughan">Dick Gaughan</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[219]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Wolfstone" title="Wolfstone">Wolfstone</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[220]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boys_of_the_Lough" title="Boys of the Lough">Boys of the Lough</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[221]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/The_Silencers_(band)" title="The Silencers (band)">The Silencers</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[222]</a></sup> have kept Scottish folk vibrant and fresh by mixing traditional Scottish and Gaelic folk songs with more contemporary genres.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[223]</a></sup> These artists have also been commercially successful in continental Europe and North America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[224]</a></sup> There is an emerging wealth of talent in the Scottish traditional music scene, with bands such as <a href="/wiki/M%C3%A0nran" title="Mànran">Mànran</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225"><a href="#cite_note-225">[225]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Skipinnish" title="Skipinnish">Skipinnish</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[226]</a></sup> Barluath<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[227]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Breabach" title="Breabach">Breabach</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[228]</a></sup> and solo artists such as Patsy Reid,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229"><a href="#cite_note-229">[229]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Robyn_Stapleton" title="Robyn Stapleton">Robyn Stapleton</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230"><a href="#cite_note-230">[230]</a></sup> and Mischa MacPherson<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[231]</a></sup> gaining a lot of success in recent years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-232"><a href="#cite_note-232">[232]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Central_and_Eastern_Europe">Central and Eastern Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Folk_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Central and Eastern Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Belarus" title="Music of Belarus">Music of Belarus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_folk_music" title="Hungarian folk music">Hungarian folk music</a>, <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Moldova" title="Music of Moldova">Music of Moldova</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russian_traditional_music" title="Russian traditional music">Russian traditional music</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_folk_music" title="Ukrainian folk music">Ukrainian folk music</a>, <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Slovenia" title="Music of Slovenia">Music of Slovenia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Czech_folklore" title="Czech folklore">Czech folklore</a></div>
<p>During the Communist era national folk dancing in the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Bloc" title="Eastern Bloc">Eastern Bloc</a> was actively promoted by the state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-233"><a href="#cite_note-233">[233]</a></sup> Dance troupes from Russia and Poland toured non-communist Europe from about 1937 to 1990.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-234"><a href="#cite_note-234">[234]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Alexandrov_Ensemble" title="Alexandrov Ensemble">Red Army Choir</a> recorded many albums, becoming the most popular military band.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-235"><a href="#cite_note-235">[235]</a></sup> Eastern Europe is also the origin of the Jewish <a href="/wiki/Klezmer" title="Klezmer">Klezmer</a> tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[236]</a></sup>
</p>
